</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="Abs1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p id="Par1">Organismal adaptations to spaceflight have been characterized at the molecular level in model organisms, including <em>Drosophila</em> and <em>C. elegans</em>. Here, we extend molecular work to energy metabolism and sex hormone signaling in mice and humans. We found spaceflight induced changes in insulin and estrogen signaling in rodents and humans. Murine changes were most prominent in the liver, where we observed inhibition of insulin and estrogen receptor signaling with concomitant hepatic insulin resistance and steatosis. Based on the metabolic demand, metabolic pathways mediated by insulin and estrogen vary among muscles, specifically between the soleus and extensor digitorum longus. In humans, spaceflight induced changes in insulin and estrogen related genes and pathways. Pathway analysis demonstrated spaceflight induced changes in insulin resistance, estrogen signaling, stress response, and viral infection. These data strongly suggest the need for further research on the metabolic and reproductive endocrinologic effects of space travel, if we are to become a successful interplanetary species.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Subject terms:</strong> Systems biology, Computational biology and bioinformatics</p></section></section><section class="abstract" id="Abs2"><hr class="headless">
<p id="Par2">Analysis of data from mice having spent time at the International Space Station and from a group of astronauts and a set of commercial spaceflight participants reveals alterations in genes related to insulin and estrogen signaling during spaceflight</p></section><section id="Sec1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Introduction</h2>
<p id="Par3">We have entered an era in which living and working in space is a reality. Studies show that spaceflight has health risks such as central nervous system and cardiovascular dysfunction, cancer risk, immune dysregulation, muscle and bone loss, and fatty liver<sup><a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">1</a></sup>. At the molecular level, risks include oxidative stress, DNA damage, cosmic radiation exposure, mitochondrial dysfunction, microbiome shift, and epigenetic alterations<sup><a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">1</a></sup>. Of these, mitochondrial dysfunction and oxidative stress have been identified as major space-related risks<sup><a href="#CR2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR2">2</a></sup>. Mitochondria are not only the powerhouse of the cell but also the center of many metabolic pathways. Insulin is a highly conserved anabolic and mitogenic peptide hormone that acts as a master regulator of energy metabolism across species, including humans<sup><a href="#CR3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR3">3</a></sup>. It is secreted by the pancreas in response to increased plasma glucose, and exerts its effects on most organs including the kidney and liver as well as skeletal muscle, and bone<sup><a href="#CR4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR4">4</a></sup>. Insulin receptor is a transmembrane receptor belonging to the family of receptor tyrosine kinases (RTKs)<sup><a href="#CR5" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR5">5</a></sup> and undergoes autophosphorylation upon activation, leading to a downstream signaling cascade which influences cellular function, particularly glucose metabolism. Insulin resistance (IR) arises when these downstream molecular pathways do not respond appropriately in target tissues, thus impeding the physiologic response to insulin. In the physiological state, IR can also be observed due to opposing actions from counterregulatory hormones such as glucagon, glucocorticoids, and catecholamines. Insulin resistance is further associated with metabolic abnormalities such as diabetes, lipid dysfunction, nonalcoholic fatty liver disease (NAFLD), cardiovascular disease, retinopathy, and polycystic ovary syndrome (PCOS). Each of these factors is also interconnected, thus resulting in a potentially vicious cycle.</p>
<p id="Par4">The presence of insulin resistance during spaceflight has been reported, and sex differences play a role, with female astronauts displaying better Homeostatic Model Assessment for Insulin Resistance (HOMA-IR) values than male astronauts<sup><a href="#CR6" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR6">6</a>,<a href="#CR7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR7">7</a></sup>. Sex differences play a role in diseases and metabolism both on Earth and in space<sup><a href="#CR8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR8">8</a></sup>. Some of these differences may be attributed to sex hormones. Estrogens play a variety of roles and serve many functions in women’s lives. Besides being a reproductive hormone, estrogen is involved in metabolism as well as in the cardiovascular, nervous, and immune systems<sup><a href="#CR9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR9">9</a></sup>. Estrogen signaling activates membrane and nuclear receptors in both canonical and non-canonical ways to modulate metabolism, and promote growth, proliferation, and differentiation of diverse cell types<sup><a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">10</a>,<a href="#CR11" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR11">11</a></sup>. Estrogen receptors are ESR1, estrogen receptor beta (ESR2), and G-protein coupled estrogen receptor 1 (GPER1). There are also estrogen-related receptors alpha, beta and gamma (ESRRA, ESRRB, ESRRG) which are structurally similar to ESR1 and ESR2<sup><a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">12</a></sup> but whose ligands are unknown. ESRRA also has DNA sequence homology to the estrogen receptor and can be activated by phytoestrogens (i.e. genistein)<sup><a href="#CR13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR13">13</a></sup>. These receptors play pivotal roles in metabolism, insulin resistance, NAFLD, and mitochondrial function<sup><a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">12</a></sup>. Besides that, an unbalanced ratio of these receptors may have an impact on metabolism<sup><a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">14</a></sup>. Additionally, estrogen and insulin combine in controlling metabolic pathways. In animal models, knockout of the estrogen receptor alpha (<em>ERα</em>) (<em>ESR1</em>) in both male and female mice results in insulin resistance and dysglycemia<sup><a href="#CR15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR15">15</a>–<a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup>. Estrogen receptors promote pancreatic cell survival, enhance insulin production, and improve insulin sensitivity<sup><a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">18</a>,<a href="#CR19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR19">19</a></sup>. Female and male <em>ESR1</em>−/− mice both display reduced GLUT4 expression, further emphasizing the role of estrogen in glycemic control<sup><a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">14</a></sup>. Clinically, compared to premenopausal women, postmenopausal women have an increased risk of adverse metabolic profiles such as type 2 diabetes, insulin resistance, impaired glucose and lipid profiles, increased android fat deposition, sarcopenia, and osteoporosis<sup><a href="#CR20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR20">20</a></sup>. Estrogens also positively correlate with bone and muscle mass and are protective against disuse atrophy<sup><a href="#CR21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR21">21</a></sup>. Thus, there is substantial reason to believe that insulin and estrogen signaling are key pathways regulating health in space.</p>
<p id="Par5">The amount of knowledge in the literature about how insulin and estrogen signaling and related pathways change during spaceflight is currently sparse. Our preliminary findings reported possible insulin and estrogen signaling alteration during spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR22">22</a>,<a href="#CR23" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR23">23</a></sup>. A few published studies show insulin resistance parameters may change during spaceflight but data are conflicting, suggesting either decreased insulin or no change<sup><a href="#CR24" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR24">24</a>,<a href="#CR25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR25">25</a></sup>. Similarly, data on estrogen signaling are conflicting with one study revealing downregulation of <em>ESR1</em> and <em>ESR2</em> in the uterus<sup><a href="#CR26" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR26">26</a></sup> whereas a recent study found no alterations in estrogen receptor expression in rodent ovaries<sup><a href="#CR27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR27">27</a></sup>. To address these gaps in our knowledge of insulin and estrogen signaling in response to spaceflight we examined these signaling pathways across multiple tissues in rodents from the same mission as well as in a group of astronauts and a set of commercial spaceflight participants/astronauts. Our results indicate significant changes in these pathways in response to spaceflight in both mice and people. These observations lay the foundation for further studies aimed at understanding the metabolic alterations induced by spaceflight as well as highlighting insulin and estrogen signaling as potential targets for countermeasures in future spaceflight missions.</p></section><section id="Sec2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Results</h2>
<section id="Sec3"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Spaceflight demonstrates global impact of insulin and estrogen signals across different organs in female mice</h3>
<p id="Par6">To understand how insulin and estrogen signaling change during spaceflight we examined gene expression in mice that were flown to the International Space Station (ISS) for 37 days. For this purpose, adrenal glands (OSD-98), kidney (OSD-102), liver (OSD-168), eyes (OSD-100), and skeletal muscles (i.e. quadriceps (OSD-103), gastrocnemius (OSD-101), soleus (OSD-104), tibialis anterior (OSD-105), and extensor digitorum longus (OSD-99)) were utilized (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1a</a>). The transcriptomic data (RNA-seq) was obtained for 16-week-old female mice from the NASA GeneLab platform<sup><a href="#CR28" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR28">28</a></sup> and were analyzed for insulin signaling, estrogen signaling and insulin resistance related genes and pathways. The gene lists for these hormones were obtained from the NCBI resource<sup><a href="#CR29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR29">29</a></sup> (i.e. ‘insulin signaling’ mus musculus). The greatest number of differentially expressed genes (DEGs) were observed in the mouse liver (<em>n</em> = 3670), followed by soleus (<em>n</em> = 3518) and extensor digitorum longus (EDL) (<em>n</em> = 1859) muscles. In all, 2677 DEGs were unique to the liver, 2048 DEGs were unique to soleus muscle, and 784 DEGs were unique to EDL (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1b</a>). The least number of DEGs was noted in the adrenal gland (<em>n</em> = 61) and 28 of them were unique to it (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1b</a>). There are a total of 1970 unique genes from the NCBI curated gene list which are commonly encountered across insulin resistance, insulin signaling and estrogen signaling pathways. Most of the genes from the NCBI list related to insulin resistance, insulin signaling, and estrogen signaling were downregulated in the liver (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1c</a>). In total, 645 out of the 1970 unique genes from the NCBI resource had gene expression values across all nine tissues. These genes were divided into seven biologically relevant clusters associated with insulin resistance and signaling (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1d</a>). In particular, the last cluster (cluster 7) at the bottom of the heatmap were related to Gene Ontology (GO) processes<sup><a href="#CR30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR30">30</a></sup> such as lipid localization and glycerolipid metabolic processes which are related to obesity which in turn can lead to a decrease in insulin sensitivity<sup><a href="#CR31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR31">31</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">1</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig1"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 1. Unique differentially expressed genes across tissues in mice.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig1_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/9e33a3fb1e48/42003_2023_5213_Fig1_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e810" height="423" width="800" alt="Fig. 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Graphical abstract showing the samples and analysis created in BioRender. <strong>b</strong> UpSet plot showing the number of differentially expressed genes (FC cutoff of 1.2 and <em>p</em>-adj&lt;0.05) that are unique to each tissue and overlapping different tissues. The liver had the highest number of unique genes while adrenal had the lowest. The two genes that were common across all nine tissues were related to circadian rhythm and insulin signaling. <strong>c</strong> Global gene level heatmap of 1970 unique genes from NCBI curated gene list which are common across insulin resistance, insulin signaling and estrogen signaling pathways. Genes are the rows and columns are the nine tissues analyzed from the RR1 mission. Genes were further divided into being unique to only one of these pathways or a combination of the pathways. Heatmap shows the <em>z</em>-scores of logFC values from the nine tissues and the tissues are clustered using hierarchical clustering. Liver was the most affected with genes being downregulated across all pathways when comparing flight vs pre-flight. <strong>d</strong> Heatmap of 645 unique genes from NCBI curated gene list which have log fold change values across all 9 tissues. Hierarchical clustering of genes shows clusters being related to GO biological processes relevant to insulin resistance, insulin signaling and estrogen signaling (Fig. <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">S1</a>). <strong>e</strong> Venn diagram of insulin resistance, insulin signaling and estrogen signaling pathways with their corresponding unique and overlapping genes from the NCBI curated gene list. There are a total of 145 genes that are common across all three pathways.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par7">In general, two genes were differentially expressed (i.e., upregulated) across all tissues in the RR-1 samples; <em>ARNTL</em> and <em>NPAS2</em>. Across skeletal muscles, 21 DEGs were commonly encountered, out of which 11 DEGs were upregulated and 9 DEGs were downregulated. <em>UCKL1OS</em> was only upregulated in soleus. Many of them were related to the circadian clock, such as <em>NR1D2, NPAS2, PER2, PER3, CIART, DBP, ARNTL</em>, and <em>SOX4</em>. In terms of insulin signaling linked genes, in muscles, <em>ARNTL, MT1, SOX4</em> were upregulated and <em>DBP</em> and <em>PER2</em> were downregulated. The genes associated with circadian rhythm, indicates the global effect of spaceflight on the circadian clock and its association with insulin signaling. Overall, from our analysis, the liver was the most impacted organ by spaceflight, with the highest number of DEGs, alongside insulin and estrogen receptor signaling predicted to be inhibited (<em>z</em>-scores: −3.772 and −5.859, respectively).</p></section><section id="Sec4"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Common insulin and estrogen signaling genes altered by spaceflight in female rodents</h3>
<p id="Par8">To determine if there are any universally shared insulin and estrogen pathways being regulated throughout the different organs during spaceflight, we performed further analysis on the 145 shared genes linked to insulin and estrogen signaling as well as insulin resistance (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1e</a>). 56 out of 145 common genes across the insulin resistance and insulin signaling pathways had gene expression values across all nine tissues. The genes were clustered into three clusters where the top enriched pathways from GO processes were: triglyceride-rich lipoprotein particle remodeling for cluster 1, positive regulation of insulin secretion for cluster 2, and carbohydrate homeostasis for cluster 3 (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2a</a>). The disease enrichment analysis revealed that 62 out of 145 genes were associated with diseases, with the top enriched disease being diabetes mellitus, followed by fatty liver (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2b</a>). Human genes at the intersection of these pathways were used in a similar manner, and the top enriched disease was obesity, followed by hypertension (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2c</a>). Thus, enriched diseases in both species emphasize the importance of insulin and estrogen signaling in metabolic diseases.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig2"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 2. Insulin resistance, insulin and estrogen signaling related genes and diseases.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig2_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/738759bcc6c1/42003_2023_5213_Fig2_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e873" height="518" width="800" alt="Fig. 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Heatmap for 56 out of 145 common genes across the insulin resistance and insulin signaling pathways for which there were gene expression values across all nine tissues. <strong>b</strong> Disease enrichment plot showing top 10 diseases from mouse genome database (MGD) being enriched using 145 unique genes common across insulin resistance, insulin signaling and estrogen signaling. The y axis shows the disease labels and the x axis shows the ratio of disease relevant genes to the total number of genes found from the 145 genes that were present in MGD. The dots in the enrichment plots are colored by the FDR values and the sizes of the dots indicate the number of genes found to be enriched for a disease. Diabetes and fatty liver are the top two enriched diseases which are related to estrogen and insulin signaling. <strong>c</strong> Disease enrichment plot is shown with top 10 diseases from human curated sources (e.g., UniProt, ClinGen) using species relevant genes out of 145 genes from the NCBI list. Obesity and fatty liver are the top two enriched diseases which are also related to estrogen and insulin signaling.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par9">Further pathway analysis utilizing gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA), focused on specific insulin and estrogen pathways, demonstrated differences in the enrichment of gene sets across organs (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>). The majority of gene sets related to insulin and insulin resistance were predicted to be inhibited in the liver (OSD-168) such as insulin receptor signaling pathway, response to troglitazone and adipogenesis (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>). Kidney (OSD-102) and eye (OSD-100) also showed similar inhibition for insulin pathways, such as insulin pathway, insulin receptor signaling pathway, response to insulin (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3A</a>). Interestingly, a set of muscle tissues (i.e. tibialis anterior (OSD-105) and EDL (OSD-99)) were shown to be activated for response to insulin, while the quadriceps (OSD-103) and gastrocnemius (OSD-101) showed negative enrichment scores for response to insulin (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>). It is known that during spaceflight the soleus muscle is impacted first with muscle mass loss which can lead to downstream dysregulation related to metabolic, inflammatory, and immune functions<sup><a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">32</a></sup>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig3"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 3. Gene set enrichment analysis of insulin and estrogen pathways.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig3_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/dfdf30e48401/42003_2023_5213_Fig3_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e915" height="445" width="800" alt="Fig. 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig3/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> A heatmap of the normalized enrichment scores (NES) of estrogen and insulin pathways impacted by spaceflight in datasets of RR-1 mission samples. The dark gray locations in the heatmap indicate missing values for the NES, resulting from off-range adjusted p-values (padj) at which the NES is obtained. The assumed range is padj &lt; 0.3. A positive NES indicates activation of the pathways, while at negative NES the pathway is inhibited. In the pathways, the full meaning of CRGS is ‘Cellular Response to Glucose Stimulus’; and the full meaning of IERSP is ‘Intracellular Estrogen Receptor Signaling Pathway’. The pathways are categorized into several types, and the MSigDB database from which the pathway is sourced is indicated in parentheses. <strong>b</strong> A heatmap of the t-score of the overlapping leading-edge pathway genes across the datasets of the RR-1 mission samples. The dark gray locations in the heatmap indicate the absence of a value for the <em>t</em>-score, resulting from off-range adjusted <em>p</em>-values (padj) at which the <em>t</em>-score is obtained. The assumed range is padj &lt; 0.05. The leading-edge genes are the genes that contribute to the GSEA predicted pathways. The overlapping leading-edge genes are commonly leading-edge genes to at least four of the datasets.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par10">Overall, there are similar organ dependent patterns for estrogen-specific pathways observed (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>). Estrogen metabolism and signaling pathways were negatively enriched in the kidney (OSD-102), liver (OSD-168), and quadriceps muscle (OSD-103). Specific estrogen signaling pathways, such as pubertal breast, were negatively enriched in the liver, eye, and adrenal gland, while being positively enriched in the (OSD-99) and tibialis anterior (OSD-105) muscles (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3a</a>).</p>
<p id="Par11">When analyzing the specific leading-edge genes involved in the insulin and estrogen pathway predictions, we observed that a subset of these genes were related to the circadian rhythm (i.e., A<em>RNTL, CLOCK, CRY2, PER3, NFIL3</em>, and <em>NR1D2</em>) (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3b</a>). The majority of these gene expressions were in the same direction across organs; however, some unique differences were observed. <em>PFKM</em> was upregulated only in the soleus muscle (OSD-104) (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3b</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec5"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Gene expression changes and proteomics suggest underlying insulin resistance in spaceflight-induced hepatosteatosis</h3>
<p id="Par12">Notably, the liver is a key metabolic organ that orchestrates and regulates a plethora of metabolic pathways, including estrogen and insulin signaling<sup><a href="#CR33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR33">33</a></sup>. During spaceflight, the liver RNA-seq data exhibits the most pronounced and unique impact on metabolic pathways as well as insulin and estrogen signaling. A comprehensive analysis of liver samples harvested after 37 days of spaceflight, revealed that both insulin and estrogen receptor signaling are predicted to be inhibited in the liver during spaceflight, as compared to ground controls (<em>z</em>-scores: −3.772 and −5.859, respectively). Our findings indicate that several genes of the insulin signaling pathway denoted development of insulin resistance and hepatic steatosis (<em>z</em>-scores: 2.481 and 3.737, respectively). Furthermore, insulin receptor was downregulated in the liver during spaceflight, along with inhibition of mitochondrial fatty acid oxidation (FAO), which may indicate derangement of mitochondrial activity. In addition, disruption of estrogen signaling has been extensively reported to aggravate hepatic insulin resistance<sup><a href="#CR33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR33">33</a>,<a href="#CR34" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR34">34</a></sup>. According to this evidence, our analysis showed inhibition of estrogen receptor signaling in the liver during spaceflight (<em>z</em>-score: −5.859). Remarkably, genes associated with mitochondrial complex I, complex II, and ATP synthase were downregulated in the estrogen signaling network (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4a</a>). This is particularly relevant considering that estrogen receptors play a key role in mitochondrial activity and biogenesis<sup><a href="#CR35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR35">35</a>–<a href="#CR37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR37">37</a></sup>. In livers harvested from mice after spaceflight, we observed impairment of estrogen signaling, which in turn may hamper oxidative phosphorylation by mainly affecting complex I (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4a</a>)<sup><a href="#CR38" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR38">38</a></sup>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig4"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 4. Hepatic steatosis in mice liver.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig4_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/f5e3edc290d3/42003_2023_5213_Fig4_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1002" height="627" width="800" alt="Fig. 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig4/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> The hepatic steatosis canonical pathway with the significantly regulated genes for liver tissue from mice flown to space compared to ground control mice. The lower panel is the mitochondrial complexes and the significantly regulated genes involved visualized by ShinyGo v0.76.3 and KEGG pathway diagrams. The boxes highlighted in yellow indicate genes related to estrogen receptor signaling. Panel was created in BioRender. <strong>b</strong> Lollipop plot illustrating the normalized enrichment scores (NES) of hepatic steatosis pathways in OSD-168 (liver tissue). Only pathways with a NES obtained at an adjusted <em>p</em>-value &lt;0.3 (padj &lt; 0.3) are shown. <strong>c</strong> An illustration of common leading-edge genes across pathways of insulin and estrogen in a heatmap of the normalized enrichment scores (NES) of the pathways impacted by spaceflight in OSD-168 (liver tissue). Only pathways with NES at padj &lt; 0.3 are shown, and only genes that are among leading edge genes seven (7) or more of the pathways are shown.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par13">Specific analysis of pathways related to hepatic steatosis (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4b, c</a>) revealed that the SREBP pathway was one of the top positively enriched pathways (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4b</a>). This pathway is known to be involved in metabolic-associated chronic liver diseases such as Nonalcoholic Fatty Liver Disease (NAFLD)<sup><a href="#CR39" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR39">39</a></sup>. Furthermore, response to insulin and insulin signaling pathways were universally enriched in hepatic steatosis during spaceflight, with insulin receptor (<em>INSR</em>) and downstream targets (i.e <em>IRS1</em>, <em>AKT1</em>, <em>AKT2</em>) among the top enriched genes for hepatic steatosis (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4c</a>). Among the top leading genes, we additionally identified <em>MFN2</em>, which encodes for Mitofusin 2, a mitochondrial fusion protein associated with insulin signaling and oxidative stress in NAFLD<sup><a href="#CR40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR40">40</a>,<a href="#CR41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR41">41</a></sup>. In summary, our findings suggest that during spaceflight, alterations of insulin and estrogen signaling may lead to the development of hepatic steatosis.</p>
<p id="Par14">We also performed proteomics analysis using RR-1 data, which revealed that the liver displayed the most insulin and estrogen-related protein abundance changes (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5a</a>). In the liver, proteins involved in lipid metabolism such as hormone sensitive lipase, Apoe, Sphk2 and Ldlr, showed notable changes in protein abundance. These proteins are also linked to insulin signaling, insulin resistance, hepatic steatosis and glucose metabolism disorder (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5b–e</a>). <em>Lipe</em> encodes hormone sensitive lipase (HSL) which hydrolyses lipids (i.e. triacylglycerols) in various tissues. Insulin inhibits HSL activity while counteracting hormones such as glucocorticoids, glucagon, and catecholamines activate HSL during stress or starvation<sup><a href="#CR42" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR42">42</a></sup>. Increased ApoE protein abundance was observed in the kidney, adrenal gland, and significantly in the liver (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5c, d</a>). Apolipoprotein E (ApoE) is one of the major lipoproteins in the cholesterol metabolism and it uptakes VLDL, IDL and chylomicron remnants from the circulation. It is also a major ligand for the LDL receptor. There was a significant increase in the abundance of LDL receptors in the liver, while it decreased in the adrenal gland (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5c–e</a>). In terms of estrogen signaling related proteins, we observed changes in protein abundance associated with mitochondrial complexes (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5f</a>). This was observed mostly in muscles, and the majority of these mitochondrial proteins belong to NADH dehydrogenases in complex I (i.e. <em>NDUFA5</em> encodes a subunit of complex I) (Fig. <a href="#Fig5" class="usa-link">5f</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig5"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 5. Quantitative <a href="https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/biochemistry-genetics-and-molecular-biology/proteomics" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">proteomics</a> screening of different tissues from the RR1 mission samples.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig5_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/adcbc682aced/42003_2023_5213_Fig5_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1112" height="999" width="663" alt="Fig. 5"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig5/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Heatmaps for logFC (Flight vs. Ground) of proteins across the estrogen signaling, insulin signaling, and insulin resistance pathways. Normalized protein intensity values were used to calculate the logFC through the Limma package. Columns represent datasets from different tissues in the RR1 missions, and rows correspond to proteins. The gray cells in the heatmap indicate missing values. Asterisks (*) overlaying cells denote statistical significance (<em>p</em>-value &lt; 0.05). Liver displayed the most protein abundance changes across these pathways. Protein abundance changes for (<strong>b</strong>) insulin receptor signaling, (<strong>c</strong>) insulin resistance, (<strong>d</strong>) hepatic steatosis, (<strong>e</strong>) glucose metabolism disorders, and (<strong>f</strong>) estrogen receptor signaling, were also depicted in different heatmaps. All 0 values for <strong>b</strong>–<strong>f</strong> are for proteins which were not present in the data.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="Sec6"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Alterations in insulin and estrogen linked genes in the rodent skin</h3>
<p id="Par15">Both insulin and estrogen act in the skin and contribute to important functions such as wound healing and skin integrity. Further, certain skin conditions have been associated with insulin resistance (i.e., acanthosis nigricans)<sup><a href="#CR43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR43">43</a></sup>. Thus, we utilized rodent data sets (RR-5, RR-7, MHU-2) to analyze skin gene expressions linked to insulin resistance, insulin, and estrogen signaling. We observed differences in pathway enrichment scores between female and male mice (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6a</a>). During the first 25 days of flight, estrogen and insulin signaling, as well as insulin resistance pathways, were negatively enriched in the skin of female C3H mice; however, these pathways were positively enriched during the 75 days of flight (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6a</a>). We also noted similarities and differences across strains. Within the same mission (RR-7), pathway enrichments in C3H mice varied based on flight duration, while the insulin resistance pathway was positively enriched in C57BL mice regardless of flight duration (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6a</a>). The insulin resistance pathway was positively enriched during 75 days of flight in two different strains (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6a</a>). Further, in female mice, the insulin resistance pathway was positively enriched in various strains and different missions, except in C3H strains exposed to 25 days of flight (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6a</a>). In the skin of male mice, differences in enrichment scores were observed based on the tissue (dorsal vs femoral skin) and diet (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6a</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig6"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 6. Insulin and estrogen signaling, insulin resistance pathways in skin rodent samples.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig6_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/1b17ae484a4a/42003_2023_5213_Fig6_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1153" height="616" width="800" alt="Fig. 6"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig6/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Pathway enrichment heatmap. Heatmap of normalized enrichment scores for insulin resistance, insulin signaling, and estrogen signaling pathways in spaceflight vs ground control comparisons of murine skin. Columns represent pathways, while rows represent datasets from multiple spaceflight missions, split by biological sex. Asterisks (*) overlaying cells denote statistical significance. <strong>b</strong> Gene-level heatmaps (one for each pathway). Heatmap of t-scores for highly significant (FDR ≤ 0.01) differentially expressed genes associated with the insulin and estrogen signaling as well as insulin resistance. Columns represent datasets from multiple spaceflight missions, and rows correspond to genes. Asterisks (*) overlaying cells denote statistical significance; genes must be significant in at least one of the datasets to be displayed.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par16">In female and male rodent skin samples, we found differences in gene expressions related to insulin and estrogen signaling (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6b</a>). Most of the significant up- and downregulations were observed in the male mice, especially in the dorsal skin (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6B</a>). Certain genes involved in tissue remodeling, extracellular matrix, wound healing, and collagen synthesis (i.e., <em>ACE, FN1, IGFBP5, MMP3, COL6A3, COL6A1, COL1A1, COL5A1, BGN, FASN, LOXL2</em>) were downregulated only in male mice (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6b</a>). <em>FN1</em> encodes fibronectin which is an essential component of the extracellular matrix (ECM) and participates in cell migration, adhesion, and wound repair<sup><a href="#CR44" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR44">44</a></sup>. Matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) are involved in tissue remodeling; MMP3 regulates the rate of wound healing and was downregulated in the skin of male mice<sup><a href="#CR45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR45">45</a></sup>. Further, epidermal lipids contribute to the skin’s barrier function<sup><a href="#CR46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR46">46</a></sup>. Fatty acids can be synthesized in the epidermis, and <em>FASN</em> encodes fatty acid synthase, which was downregulated in male mice. Insulin receptor (<em>INSR</em>), <em>IRS2</em>, estrogen receptor 1 (<em>ESR1</em>), and <em>ESRRG</em> were upregulated in male mice dorsal skin (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6b</a>). Estrogen promotes skin and mucosa epithelialization<sup><a href="#CR47" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR47">47</a>,<a href="#CR48" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR48">48</a></sup>. Sex differences may play a role in wound healing and variations in healing rates have been reported in literature<sup><a href="#CR49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR49">49</a>,<a href="#CR50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR50">50</a></sup>. In female mice, <em>S100s9</em>, <em>Nr4a2</em>, <em>Nr4a3</em>, <em>Il6</em>, and <em>Egf2</em> were significantly downregulated only in the skin of C3H mice during 25 days of flight (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6b</a>). Although we noticed some changes in the pathway enrichment scores, results need to be interpreted carefully, due to differences in missions, durations, strains, housing, and diet.</p></section><section id="Sec7"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Changes in insulin signaling genes and pathways conserved in astronauts</h3>
<p id="Par17">To explore if insulin and estrogen signaling-related genes are affected in astronauts during spaceflight, human samples were used to map genes of interest. We utilized astronaut data from the Japanese Space Agency (JAXA) Cell-Free Epigenome (CFE) Study mission<sup><a href="#CR51" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR51">51</a></sup> with astronauts in space for 120 days. Specifically, for the JAXA mission RNA-seq was conducted on plasma cell-free RNAs (cfRNAs) from 6 astronauts that were on the International Space Station (ISS) for 120 days from pre-flight, during flight, and post flight time points.</p>
<p id="Par18">Insulin signaling linked genes are dysregulated during and after spaceflight in astronauts (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). Some genes were specifically upregulated within the first month upon return (R + 3d, R + 30d) such as <em>AVP, FFAR4, GLP1R, RIMS2, FGF6, NR1H4, PRLH, CPE</em>, and <em>APOM</em>. The <em>FFAR4</em> encodes free fatty acid receptor 4, is widely expressed in tissues and has been associated with metabolic and cardiovascular diseases<sup><a href="#CR52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR52">52</a></sup>. The <em>GLP1R</em> is another key gene encoding glucagon-like peptide (GLP-1) receptor which stimulates insulin secretion (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). Additionally, insulin secretion was among the enriched pathways (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). GLP-1 receptor agonist is currently used in the treatment of diabetes and obesity<sup><a href="#CR53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR53">53</a></sup>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig7"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 7. Insulin Signaling linked genes and pathways from JAXA Cell-Free Epigenome (CFE).</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig7_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/be1505353056/42003_2023_5213_Fig7_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1289" height="805" width="800" alt="Fig. 7"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig7/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Heatmap of the significantly upregulated genes compared to pre-flight for either flight or post-flight of normalized plasma cell-free RNA expression values and associated pathways for insulin signaling over time for the six astronauts over 120 days in space from JAXA study. The values shown on are the averaged normalized expression values for all six astronauts each time point during flight and post-flight. The four pre-flight time points were averaged together, since the changes for genes in the time leading up to flight are considered to be the same and part of the baseline values. For the time, L = Launch (i.e., meaning time after launch from Earth and length in space) and R = Return to Earth. Based on heatmap, pathway analysis performed using ShinyGO to reveal the top 30 pathways (FDR &lt; 0.05) being regulated for postflight upregulated insulin linked genes. The red bold font texts are the pathways that are directly known to regulate the insulin pathway in the pathway figure. <strong>b</strong> Heatmap and associated pathways showing insulin signaling linked downregulated genes in postflight or flight vs preflight. Based on heatmap, pathway analysis performed using ShinyGO to reveal which pathways are being regulated for downregulated insulin linked genes. The pathways and lollipop plots demonstrate top 30 pathways (based on FDR). The red bold font texts are the pathways that are directly known to regulate the insulin pathway in the pathway figure.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par19">Certain genes involved in insulin signaling were upregulated throughout the postflight period. <em>MAF</em> is a transcription factor that is known to regulate and activate insulin gene expression<sup><a href="#CR54" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR54">54</a></sup> (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). Similarly, <em>RARRES2</em>, which encodes chemerin, was also upregulated throughout postflight. Chemerin is an adipokine that is associated with insulin resistance and obesity<sup><a href="#CR55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR55">55</a></sup>. <em>AKT1</em> is the main downstream of insulin signaling and was upregulated during postflight which also correlates with the postflight PI3K/AKT enrichment (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). <em>CRH, GCGR</em>, and <em>SLC2A3</em> are other important genes related to endocrine hormones and insulin. The glucagon receptor, encoded by <em>GCGR</em>, is the primary receptor for glucagon. Glucagon counteracts the actions of insulin and has a role in stress response<sup><a href="#CR56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR56">56</a></sup>. The <em>GCGR</em> was upregulated throughout the entire postflight period (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7b</a>). <em>SLC2A3</em> encodes for a glucose transporter named GLUT3 and is widely expressed in various cells. <em>SLC2A3</em> was downregulated upon return from spaceflight (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7b</a>). The cholesterol metabolism was the top activated pathway for postflight upregulated genes involved in insulin signaling (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). Insulin secretion, regulation of lipolysis in adipocytes, Cushing syndrome, and circadian entrainment were other enriched pathways (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>).</p>
<p id="Par20">Glucagon signaling was the top activated pathway for downregulated genes associated with insulin signaling (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7b</a>). Insulin resistance, lipid and atherosclerosis, AMPK signaling, oxytocin signaling and viral infection related pathways (i.e., HPV, CMV infections, HTLV1) were other enriched pathways (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7b</a>). The <em>MS4A1</em> gene encodes CD20, a B-cell marker, and it was downregulated during postflight but upregulated during flight (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7b</a>).</p>
<p id="Par21">Further, some of the gene expressions changed at specific time points. Interestingly, <em>CRP</em> was upregulated upon return (R + 3d) and was downregulated a month later (R + 30d) (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). Follow-up samples showed upregulation of <em>CRP</em> on the second and third months postflight (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). <em>CRP</em> is also linked to estrogen signaling and insulin resistance (Figs. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8</a>a, <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9a</a>). This is important as the <em>CRP</em> gene encodes c-reactive protein, which is an inflammatory marker. Elevated CRP levels correlate with both fasting insulin and insulin resistance parameters (i.e. HOMA-IR)<sup><a href="#CR57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR57">57</a></sup>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig8"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 8. Insulin Resistant linked genes and pathways from JAXA Cell-Free Epigenome (CFE).</h4>

The values shown on are the averaged normalized expression values for all six astronauts each time point during flight and post-flight. The four pre-flight time points were averaged together, since the changes for genes in the time leading up to flight are considered to be the same and part of the baseline values.
