The pathways and lollipop plots demonstrate top 30 pathways (based on FDR). The red bold font texts are the pathways that are directly known to regulate the insulin-resistant pathway in the pathway figure.</p></figcaption></figure><figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig9"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 9. Estrogen Signaling linked genes and pathways from JAXA Cell-Free Epigenome (CFE).</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig9_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/707fb7480344/42003_2023_5213_Fig9_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1424" height="443" width="800" alt="Fig. 9"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig9/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Heatmap of the significantly upregulated genes compared to pre-flight for either flight or post-flight of normalized plasma cell-free RNA expression values and associated pathways for estrogen signaling over time for the six astronauts over 120 days in space from JAXA study. The values shown on are the averaged normalized expression values for all six astronauts each time point during flight and post-flight. The four pre-flight time points were averaged together, since the changes for genes in the time leading up to flight are considered to be the same and part of the baseline values. For the time, L = Launch (i.e., meaning time after launch from Earth and length in space) and R = Return to Earth. Based on heatmap, pathway analysis performed using ShinyGO to reveal the top 30 pathways (FDR &lt; 0.05) being regulated for postflight upregulated estrogen signaling linked genes. The red bold font texts are the pathways that are directly known to regulate the insulin pathway in the pathway figure. <strong>b</strong> Heatmap and associated pathways showing estrogen signaling linked downregulated genes in postflight or flight vs preflight. Based on heatmap, pathway analysis performed using ShinyGO to reveal which pathways are being regulated for downregulated estrogen signaling linked genes. The pathways and lollipop plots demonstrate top 30 pathways (based on FDR). The red bold font texts are the pathways that are directly known to regulate the estrogen signaling pathway in the pathway figure.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="Sec8"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Insulin Resistance related genes and pathways during and after spaceflight</h3>
<p id="Par22">The majority of insulin signaling related genes and their alterations could result in insulin resistance which would be a great concern during long-term spaceflight. <em>AKT1</em> was upregulated and <em>AKT3</em> was downregulated throughout the postflight period (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8a, b</a>). <em>AKT1</em> and <em>AKT3</em> are members of serine/threonine kinases and are downstream of insulin signaling. Insulin resistance can be seen with hormonal dysfunctions like acromegaly, hyperaldosteronism, and Cushing disease<sup><a href="#CR58" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR58">58</a></sup>. Aldosterone is responsible for fluid and sodium balance and <em>CYP11B2</em>, and encodes aldosterone synthase, which was upregulated within the first week upon return from spaceflight (R + 3d) (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8a</a>), a period that overlaps with intravascular fluid changes upon return. Other genes associated with insulin resistance are <em>MT-ND1</em> and <em>MT-ND6</em>. <em>MT-ND1</em>, and <em>MT-ND6</em> genes encode subunits of NADH dehydrogenase in mitochondria complex I. Both genes were upregulated during spaceflight (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8a, b</a>). Mitochondria are the center of many biochemical pathways, and one of them is fatty acid oxidation where insulin and estrogen exert their effects. <em>CD36</em> and <em>CPT1A</em> are other key genes in fatty acid metabolism and were upregulated during the various phases of spaceflight (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8b</a>). The CD36 is responsible for fatty acid uptake into cells and is associated with insulin resistance and cardiovascular disease. <em>LEPR, APP, CXCL5, LYPLAL1, GHL3</em> are other upregulated insulin resistance related genes during flight (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8b</a>).</p>
<p id="Par23">In the pathway analysis, postflight upregulated genes linked to insulin resistance, showed enrichment of endocrine resistance, estrogen signaling, cholesterol metabolism, regulation of lipolysis in adipocytes, and maturity onset diabetes of the young (MODY) pathways (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8a</a>). Downregulated genes in flight or postflight vs preflight are given in Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8b</a>. There was a trend of upregulation of these genes during spaceflight (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8b</a>). These genes showed enriched pathways of insulin resistance, type II diabetes mellitus, AGE-RAGE signaling pathway in diabetic complications and diabetic cardiomyopathy (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8b</a>).</p>
<p id="Par24">The genes involved in insulin signaling and resistance from human data revolve around key cellular metabolic events such as lipid metabolism, viral infection pathways, and estrogen signaling. The endocrine-metabolic signature observed here illustrates the prominence of regulation of action of insulin during and after spaceflight. Additionally, there is a prominent immune-mediated cytokine and adipokine response to spaceflight in humans. This corroborates growth inducing actions of insulin via the prominent PI3K/Akt and the Ras/MAPK pathways.</p></section><section id="Sec9"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Estrogen signaling related genes and their pathways during and after spaceflight</h3>
<p id="Par25"><em>ESR1</em> encodes Estrogen Receptor 1 (Estrogen Receptor alpha), the primary receptor for estrogens, which is widely expressed in both rodents and humans. Estrogen signaling via this receptor can alter energy metabolism and conversely, gonadal estrogen production is regulated by the state of energy. ESR1-regulated pathways include insulin signaling, insulin resistance, leptin signaling, and fatty acid synthesis. <em>ESR1</em> was upregulated within 2 months (R + 3d, R + 30d, R + 60d) and then downregulated again in the 3rd month (R + 120d) postflight (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9a</a>). A similar trend during postflight was observed for <em>PER1</em> expressions (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9a</a>). <em>PER1</em> encodes Per1 protein, which is one of the major components of circadian rhythm. Circadian rhythm and circadian clock genes are closely related to metabolism and hormones. <em>CYP17A1</em> was upregulated during postflight, which encodes a key enzyme in steroidogenesis for cortisol and sex steroid synthesis (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9a</a>).</p>
<p id="Par26">Estrogen-related genes altered postflight, with most being upregulated throughout the postflight period (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9a</a>), included <em>ZNF423, GAL, EGFR, FST, PAX2, SLC34A1, GREB1, F5, FGF23, AKT1 and NOS1</em>. These genes are also implicated in the constitutive PIK3CA signaling pathway and myogenesis. This could be a direct effect of spaceflight-induced physical stress as nuclear-initiated estrogen signaling (NICE) can induce corticotropin-releasing hormone CRH through the induction of nitric oxide (<em>NOS1</em> upregulation) and via PI3K signaling activation<sup><a href="#CR59" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR59">59</a></sup>. Similarly, selected genes <em>CRHR2, PRKCD, TRIM24, IL6ST, MALAT1, OXTR, INPP4B, KMT2C, LEPR</em>, and <em>TAB2</em> within the downregulated cohort revealing a consistent pattern of change with spaceflight represent a niche of interleukin 6/JAK/STAT3 and MAPK signaling.</p>
<p id="Par27">Estrogen-linked upregulated genes at postflight showed that steroid hormone biosynthesis was the top activated pathway (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9a</a>). Other enriched pathways were ovarian steroidogenesis, estrogen signaling, Cushing syndrome, and retinol metabolism in favor of stress response and sex steroid synthesis. Estrogen-linked downregulated genes and their associated pathways showed activation of insulin resistance, AGE-RAGE signaling pathway in diabetic complications, lipid and atherosclerosis, and growth hormone synthesis pathways (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9b</a>). Estrogen also has a role in immunity, and enrichment of viral infection pathways like CMV, HTLV1 and HPV infections are also detected (Fig. <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9b</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec10"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Intricate dance of insulin and estrogen signaling and insulin resistance</h3>
<p id="Par28">Some of the genes in the human samples were related to both insulin and estrogen signaling as well as insulin resistance including <em>IGFBP1, FGF23, WNT5A, CRP, ESR1, EGFR, FST, NCOR1, CD36, CYP17A1, SHBG, KISS1, LEPR</em>. The leptin receptor (LEPR) and its signaling are crucial for metabolism and obesity<sup><a href="#CR31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR31">31</a></sup>. <em>LEPR</em> was downregulated upon return from spaceflight, regardless of the time interval (Figs. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7</a>b, <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8</a>b, and <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9b</a>). The <em>CYP17A1</em> gene was upregulated upon return (Fig. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7a</a>). <em>CYP17A1</em>, encodes steroidogenic enzymes, 17 alpha-hydroxylase, and 17,20 lyase. These enzymes are responsible for the production of cortisol, estrogen and androgens, and their deficiencies have been linked to both hormonal imbalance and metabolic dysfunction such as hypertension. Most hormones bind to proteins in the circulation. Sex hormone binding globulin (SHBG) binds sex steroid hormones (estrogen and testosterone), and because of this interaction, the amount of SHBG is important as decreasing SHBG levels may lead to increasing free testosterone and estradiol levels, and vice versa. <em>SHGB</em> was upregulated 1 month upon return (R + 30d) and downregulated at the 3rd month (Figs. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7</a>a, <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8</a>a, and <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9a</a>). Overlapping pathways were also observed such as PI3K-Akt signaling, cAMP signaling, insulin resistance, and estrogen signaling. It is well known that changes in estrogen signaling have been associated with insulin resistance<sup><a href="#CR60" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR60">60</a></sup>, as seen in our results showing the enrichment of the estrogen signaling pathway via insulin resistance linked genes. Viral infection related pathways are enriched with both insulin and estrogen downregulated genes (Figs. <a href="#Fig7" class="usa-link">7</a>b and <a href="#Fig9" class="usa-link">9b</a>).</p></section><section id="Sec11"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Insulin and estrogen signaling changes are observed in multiple human missions</h3>
<p id="Par29">To explore if sex differently impacted insulin signaling, estrogen signaling, and insulin resistance, we utilized astronaut data (2 female and 2 male) from the first civilian commercial 3-day space mission, referred to as Inspiration4 (i4)<sup><a href="#CR61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR61">61</a></sup>. From the i4 mission, single-cell gene expression data from peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMCs) and skin spatial transcriptomics data were generated.</p>
<p id="Par30">Genes in the estrogen signaling, insulin signaling, and insulin resistance overlapped less than 10% with DEGs at R + 1 from PBMCs and subpopulations (CD4 T, CD8 T, other T, B, NK, CD14 Mono, CD16 Mono, DC) (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10a</a>). Among these overlapping genes, the percentage of the upregulated genes were higher than downregulated genes (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10a</a>). Log<sub>2</sub> Female DEGs to Male DEGs ratios varied across cell types, based on insulin and estrogen linked gene categories. Log<sub>2</sub> Female DEGs to Male DEGs ratios were positive in most cell types for insulin signaling (i.e. PBMCs, CD4 T, CD8 T, NK, DC, and ‘other’ subpopulations) (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10b</a>). Certain cell types showed either a positive or negative ratio, depending on the hormone of interest. For instance, in females, in terms of DC, the DEGs ratio was positive for insulin-linked genes, but negative for estrogen-linked genes. In estrogen signaling, downregulated genes of B and other cell types were higher in females (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10b</a>). In CD4 T cells, the ratios were positive in insulin, estrogen signaling and IR linked genes (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10b</a>). We also calculated insulin resistance, estrogen signaling, and insulin signaling scores to track the changes over spaceflight. Higher enrichment scores were observed in all signaling pathways on R + 82, in comparison to R + 1 (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10c</a>). In general, there was not a strong sex difference across cells (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10c</a>). In certain cell types, differences in enrichment scores were observed depending on mission timeline and sex (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10c</a>). In dendritic cells (DC), three pathways were positively enriched on R + 82 in both sexes; however, enrichment scores were different among females and males on R + 45 (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10c</a>). T cell metabolism is modulated through insulin receptor signaling during inflammation and infection<sup><a href="#CR62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR62">62</a></sup>. Alternatively, energy consumption due to T cell activation drives upregulation of insulin receptors<sup><a href="#CR63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR63">63</a></sup>. Therefore, it is evident that insulin signaling is a key immune-metabolic event during increased physiologic demands, as in i4 cohort findings. Similarly, estrogen receptor alpha is known to mediate T cell-dependent inflammatory reactions in a diseased state<sup><a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">12</a></sup>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig10"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 10. Insulin and estrogen signaling in inspiration4 astronaut PBMC and skin samples.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11166981_42003_2023_5213_Fig10_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/27f4/11166981/c3c048427e35/42003_2023_5213_Fig10_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d33e1694" height="463" width="800" alt="Fig. 10"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig10/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>a</strong> Percent overlap of genes associated with insulin and estrogen signaling in single cell RNA-seq PBMC data, by cell types. <strong>b</strong> Log2 Female to male gene ratios from panel <strong>c</strong>. Fold changes of enrichment scores in response to spaceflight by sex, postflight collection time points, and cell types, PBMC data. The color scale bar represents fold change values are relative to pre-flight. <strong>d</strong> Fold changes of enrichment scores in postflight relative to preflight time point, by sex and tissue compartments (OE outer epidermis, IE inner epidermis, OD outer dermis, VA vasculature) in skin biopsy data. The color scale bar represents fold change values.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par31">We also compared these findings in human skin data. To perform spatial transcriptomics analysis, we compared transcript profiles of preflight (L-44) and postflight (R + 1) tissue sections across four compartments within the skin, including outer epidermis (OE), inner epidermis (IE), outer dermis (OD) and vasculature (VA). While statistical analysis is limited by small sample sizes, we found slight differences between male and female skin compartments. Using the ssGSEA approach, overall, we observed increased enrichments in insulin resistance genes and decreased enrichments in estrogen and insulin signaling genes. There were slight sex differences, and the degrees of variation were region-specific. For example, we saw the strongest loss and sex-dependent variation of estrogen and insulin signaling signatures in VA regions (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10d</a>). Insulin resistance linked genes were more enriched in females in the postflight vascular compartment and outer epidermis relative to preflight samples. In inner epidermis, both insulin and estrogen-signaling-linked genes showed larger changes in enrichments in females than in males.</p></section></section><section id="Sec12"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Discussion</h2>
<p id="Par32">Consistent with past reports that insulin signaling is a central regulator of gene expression changes and lipid metabolism in response to spaceflight in the worm, <em>C. elegans</em><sup><a href="#CR64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR64">64</a>,<a href="#CR65" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR65">65</a></sup>, we find that gene expression changes related to insulin signaling occur in spaceflown rodents and humans. Further, we find that estrogen signaling gene expression changes also occur in both spaceflown rodents and humans.</p>
<p id="Par33">In mice, the liver and soleus muscle were the tissues that displayed the largest changes in global gene expression (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1b</a>). These tissues also displayed large changes in insulin and estrogen signaling gene expression (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1c, d</a>). In the liver, insulin receptor (<em>INSR</em>) and downstream kinases <em>AKT1</em> and <em>AKT2</em> were downregulated during spaceflight. These changes were accompanied by decreased expression of other insulin receptor signaling genes. The metabolic pathways directly controlled by insulin were inhibited, which resulted in activation of dysglycemia, insulin resistance, and hepatic steatosis. This last prediction is particularly striking given the increase in liver mass in these mice in a prior study utilizing the same cohort<sup><a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup>. Hepatic steatosis is a direct consequence of insulin resistance and can in turn exacerbate insulin resistance, leading to the development of a metabolic syndrome, and thus resulting in a vicious cycle<sup><a href="#CR67" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR67">67</a></sup>. Prior studies have reported that during spaceflight, mice developed hepatomegaly<sup><a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup> and hepatic lipid accumulation<sup><a href="#CR68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR68">68</a>,<a href="#CR69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR69">69</a></sup>. Further, the livers used in our analysis showed histologic evidence of lipid accumulation via Oil Red O staining in a prior study<sup><a href="#CR68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR68">68</a></sup>. Thus, our results suggest that disrupted insulin signaling may underlie these changes. In addition to the insulin signaling changes, the impaired estrogen signaling we observed (z-score &lt; −2), may contribute to hepatic insulin resistance.</p>
<p id="Par34">In rodents, both male and female estrogen receptor knockout mice display insulin resistance<sup><a href="#CR15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR15">15</a></sup>. Further, both insulin and estrogen actively play a role in metabolism and our findings also suggest that mitochondrial dysfunction, previously reported during spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR2">2</a></sup>, may be due to alterations in insulin and estrogen signaling (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4a</a>). PGC-1α, encoded by <em>PPARGA1A</em>, is a master regulator in metabolism and mitochondrial biogenesis<sup><a href="#CR70" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR70">70</a></sup>. <em>PPARGC1A</em> downregulation in the liver was observed in both insulin and estrogen gene expression changes. Additionally, estrogen regulates mitochondrial function and biogenesis<sup><a href="#CR35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR35">35</a>–<a href="#CR37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR37">37</a></sup>. In our liver data, downregulation of mitochondrial complex I, complex 2, and ATP synthase genes were observed in the estrogen signaling network (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4a</a>). Thus, alterations in insulin and estrogen signaling in the liver may contribute to whole body alterations in metabolism in response to spaceflight.</p>
<p id="Par35">Soleus muscle was the second most affected tissue in mouse studies. Soleus is a slow twitch (type I) muscle fiber, dense in mitochondria to provide sustained oxidative capacity for long-term demands. Prior studies show atrophy in the soleus muscle after spaceflight in both rodents and humans<sup><a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup>, and differential gene expressions between soleus and EDL muscle in rodents<sup><a href="#CR71" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR71">71</a></sup>. Myostatin (<em>MSTN</em>) controls muscle mass and has been reported to be upregulated in microgravity in the soleus muscle but not in EDL<sup><a href="#CR72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR72">72</a></sup>; we confirm this observation. Other muscle atrophy related genes, <em>FBX032</em> (Atrogin-1) and <em>TRIM63</em>, and mechanoreceptor <em>PIEZO1</em> were also downregulated in the soleus. The downregulation of <em>FBX032</em> and <em>TRIM63</em> suggest the decline in soleus muscle size<sup><a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup> is not caused by increases in muscle protein degradation. This is consistent with past reports that muscle protein degradation is not increased in flight, but rather that protein synthesis declines<sup><a href="#CR73" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR73">73</a></sup>. Previous studies in astronauts have suggested that altered insulin signaling may underlie muscle atrophy<sup><a href="#CR25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR25">25</a></sup>. Further, in a prior study from the same mission, mice soleus muscle weight was decreased by 19%, despite no change in body weight and being physically active in space<sup><a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup>. Given that insulin promotes protein synthesis, our finding of decreased expression in soleus of <em>IRS2</em> (Insulin receptor substrate 2), one of major downstream insulin signaling, suggests that insulin alterations contribute to declines in protein synthesis to promote muscle atrophy in flight; this is also consistent with suggestions from spaceflown <em>C. elegans</em><sup><a href="#CR65" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR65">65</a></sup>. On the contrary, insulin receptor signaling was activated in the EDL (z-score: 2.52). Moreover, insulin-induced <em>ACACA</em>, a rate-limiting enzyme in lipogenesis, was upregulated indicating an insulin responsive state in the EDL. Further, the majority of the insulin signaling significant genes at the intersection of soleus and EDL were regulated in opposite directions, which shows distinctive insulin response in these muscles (Supplementary Data <a href="#MOESM4" class="usa-link">1</a>).</p>
<p id="Par36">Estrogen also promotes muscle growth, regeneration, insulin sensitivity, and controls muscle inflammation<sup><a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">14</a>,<a href="#CR74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR74">74</a></sup>. Estrogen-related receptor beta and gamma (<em>ESRRB, ESRRG</em>) induce mitochondrial function in myotubes and have a role in shifting muscle fibers towards oxidative types<sup><a href="#CR75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR75">75</a></sup>. Muscle-specific <em>ESR1</em> knockout mice models showed decreased oxidative metabolism, impaired mitochondrial fission and glucose metabolism<sup><a href="#CR76" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR76">76</a></sup>. Thus, our findings that <em>ESR1</em> receptor was upregulated and <em>ESRRB</em> and <em>ESRRG</em> were downregulated in soleus, while <em>ESRRA, ESRRB, ESRRG</em> were all upregulated in the EDL, suggest diverse actions of estrogen in muscle metabolism during spaceflight. Interestingly, while soleus size declined and EDL remained stable, as consistent with past studies<sup><a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup>, the gene expression changes in soleus and EDL were distinct with EDL displaying an enrichment for growth pathways (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3</a>), also consistent with prior studies<sup><a href="#CR71" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR71">71</a></sup>. Estrogen receptor signaling pathways were activated in both EDL and soleus muscles. Soleus muscle mass recovery is reported to be slower after hindlimb suspension in ovariectomized mice<sup><a href="#CR77" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR77">77</a></sup>. Additionally, increased estrogen receptor expressions were reported following muscle trauma in regenerating myofibers<sup><a href="#CR74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR74">74</a></sup>. Thus, estrogen receptor signaling activation and upregulation of <em>ESR1</em> could be a compensatory mechanism against muscle disuse in the soleus muscle, whereas, in EDL the ESR signaling activation could support muscle growth and energy metabolism. Adipose tissue is another endocrine organ which is a part of cross-talk between muscle and liver. Our results are limited to muscle and liver, as adipose tissue was not available for analysis.</p>
<p id="Par37">Studies in humans have more constraints than in model organisms, thus for the present study we were limited to blood samples and skin in spaceflown humans. However, as insulin and estrogen are circulating hormones, changes in their effects should be detected in these tissues; albeit at lower levels such as in the non-liver and non-soleus tissues in rodents (Fig. <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1</a>). As with the worm and rodent data, changes in insulin resistance related genes were detected inflight and postflight. Postflight samples from human subjects displayed upregulated insulin signaling genes (i.e., <em>AKT1, RARRES2</em>) as well as enriched insulin secretion and PI3K/AKT signaling (Fig. <a href="#Fig8" class="usa-link">8a</a>), possibly suggesting decline in these pathways inflight. Additionally, postflight upregulation of the glucagon signaling pathway may indicate underlying postflight stress and may result in insulin resistance. Overall, postflight up- and down-regulated genes associated with insulin signaling displayed enriched lipid metabolism, hormones (i.e., estrogen, growth hormone, glucagon), circadian rhythm, and immune system pathways. Also, in the human data, we found the estrogen signaling pathway was enriched with insulin related genes. <em>ESR1</em> was upregulated within 2 months (R + 3d, R + 30d, R + 60d) and then downregulated in the 3rd month (R + 120d). In correlation with this finding <em>CYP17A1</em> was upregulated postflight. These findings may explain the enriched ovarian steroidogenesis and steroid hormone synthesis pathways seen postflight. Estrogen is closely related to the immune system, as an example, Treg cell distribution changes during menstrual cycles and increases during the follicular phase<sup><a href="#CR78" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR78">78</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par38">In our data, dendritic cells in females showed higher enrichment scores in estrogen and insulin linked genes on R + 45 than male counterparts (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10c</a>). Sex difference was also observed in both rodent and human skin samples. In human skin, in the vascular region (VA) of the dermis, insulin resistance-related genes were more enriched in females during postflight while insulin and estrogen signaling-related genes were less enriched (Fig. <a href="#Fig10" class="usa-link">10d</a>). In rodent skin samples, certain genes associated with tissue remodeling and collagen synthesis (i.e. <em>COL1A1</em>, <em>COL6A3</em>, <em>COL6A1</em>, <em>COL5A1</em>) (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6b</a>) were downregulated in male mice (Fig. <a href="#Fig6" class="usa-link">6b</a>). Interestingly, increased urinary COL1A1 and COL3A1 levels have been reported in the NASA twin study<sup><a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">79</a></sup>. Insulin and estrogen both play an active role in skin homeostasis, and insulin resistance has been linked to certain skin conditions<sup><a href="#CR43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR43">43</a></sup>. Dermis is a connective tissue rich in vasculature, fibroblasts, collagen and elastic fibers, glands, hair follicles, and nerve endings. Both insulin and estrogen signaling exist in vascular endothelium and cause vasodilation via nitric oxide<sup><a href="#CR80" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR80">80</a>,<a href="#CR81" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR81">81</a></sup>. Insulin-resistant states could impair vascular endothelium<sup><a href="#CR82" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR82">82</a>,<a href="#CR83" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR83">83</a></sup>. Estrogen promotes skin and mucosal epithelialization and proliferation. Studies reported positive impact of estrogen in wound healing<sup><a href="#CR84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR84">84</a></sup>. Clinically, topical estrogen is used in the treatment of vulvovaginal atrophy, and it proliferates epithelium<sup><a href="#CR48" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR48">48</a></sup>. Although our findings are limited by sample size, differences in the enrichment scores based on sex and skin compartments warrant future research on skin conditions after spaceflight in a sex and hormone-dependent manner. Further, due to our study setting, we cannot conclude on certain space-related environmental factors, such as lunar soil and dust, and their effects on insulin and estrogen signaling in the skin.</p>
<p id="Par39">In conclusion, we find insulin and estrogen signaling are altered during and after spaceflight in rodents and people. These changes impact metabolism and forebode comorbidities. One advantage of the rodent data is that genes for pathways of interest were examined in various tissues from the same sample cohort, including mice of the same strain and age. However, a key limitation of our study is the small number of human participants. Future studies should include different rodent strains, more human subjects, and different mission profiles to gain more knowledge on these genes/pathways of interest and expand our results. Particular attention should be paid to the effects on reproductive hormone synthesis and actions, as these impact a wide range of tissues, including bone health, energy metabolism and, of course species survival via reproduction. Personalized healthcare using genomic data is the way of the future for both Earth and beyond<sup><a href="#CR85" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR85">85</a></sup>. Therefore, as we progress toward becoming an interplanetary species, further study on reproductive endocrinology and metabolism in space is essential for success.</p></section><section id="Sec13"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Methods</h2>
<section id="Sec14"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Animal and sample collection</h3>
<p id="Par40">All omics data related to mouse samples were obtained from NASA’s GeneLab public omics repository<sup><a href="#CR86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR86">86</a></sup>. The data derived from spaceflight experiments carried out onboard the ISS as part of the Rodent Research-1 (RR-1), RR-5, RR-7, and MHU-2 missions. A description of the experimental design, the corresponding type of tissues, and the data accessed are summarized in Supplementary Table <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">1</a>. All previous animal experiments and methods were performed in accordance with the relevant guidelines at each principal investigators’ institutions and were approved by the Institutional Animal Care and Use committee (IACUC). A detailed description of the methodology for obtaining tissue samples and processing is available in NASA’s GeneLab platform.</p>
<p id="Par41">For the RR-1 mission, in summary, forty female 12-week-old C57Bl/6 J (Jackson Lab, Bar Harbor, ME) were selected based on similar body weights for 4 experimental groups (16-week-old at launch, n = 10/group): baseline control, vivarium control, habitat control, and flight. Flight mice were launched onboard the ISS on 21 September 2014. Relevant to this study, the habitat control was placed in the ISS Environmental Simulator at NASA KSC on a 4-day delay to mimic flight temperature, CO<sub>2</sub>, and humidity conditions on the ISS for the duration of spaceflight. Flight mice were exposed to microgravity for a total of 37 days (33 days on ISS and 4 days in the Dragon Capsule). Mice were euthanized by injection of Euthasol followed by cervical dislocation and immediately fast frozen intact or partially dissected prior to carcasses being frozen in the Minus Eighty Degree Laboratory Freezer (MELFI) aboard the ISS. All ground mice were processed similarly. Flight mice carcasses returned to Earth February 2015 and were then maintained along with the control mice frozen at the Biospecimen Sharing Program (BSP) at the Ames Research Center until dissection<sup><a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par42">Samples were collected and processed as previously described<sup><a href="#CR27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR27">27</a>,<a href="#CR68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR68">68</a></sup>. Mouse carcasses were removed from −80 °C storage and thawed at room temperature for 15–20 min. Multiple tissues were collected from each carcass. This study was based on the following tissue types, the Adrenal glands (OSD-98), Extensor digitorum longus muscle (OSD-99), Eye (OSD-100), Gastrocnemius muscle (OSD-101), Kidney (OSD-102), Quadriceps Muscle (OSD-103), Soleus muscle (OSD-104), Tibialis anterior muscle (OSD-105), and Liver (OSD-168, OSD-48).</p></section><section id="Sec15"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">RNA sequencing and analysis</h3>
<p id="Par43">Protocol for each dataset is available on the GeneLab platform (Table <a href="#MOESM2" class="usa-link">S1</a>). Data validation and quality control was performed with FASTQC and Trim Galore! read alignment to the mouse genome using STAR RNA-seq aligner generation of gene-level expected count data with RSEM<sup><a href="#CR87" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR87">87</a></sup>. UpSet plots were generated using the UpSetR package in R. The UpSet plot bars were sorted from the largest number of genes to the smallest number of genes in each intersection set with each set having ≥ 2 genes. Heat maps were created using packages available through R (pheatmap and complexheatmap). Hierarchical clustering was used for the heatmaps where Euclidean distance was used as a distance measure and the complete linkage method was used to find similar clusters.</p>
<p id="Par44">For the gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) Pathway Analysis, the Genelab datasets are re-analyzed to produce the t-score. The Differential Expression (DE) analysis is accomplished using DESeq2 version 1.26.0<sup><a href="#CR88" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR88">88</a></sup>, in R software version 4.1.2. Expected counts from the RSEM step were extracted and rounded up to the next integer and used as input for DE analysis. The ensembl IDs of the genes in the DE datasets were annotated to their corresponding gene symbols using the R package biomaRt<sup><a href="#CR89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR89">89</a>,<a href="#CR90" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR90">90</a></sup>. The murine skin datasets were analyzed via a similar pipeline available at <a href="https://github.com/henrycope/spaceflight-skin-transcriptomics-insulin-estrogen" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://github.com/henrycope/spaceflight-skin-transcriptomics-insulin-estrogen</a>, using R version 4.1.0, DESeq2 version 1.32, and biomaRt version 2.48.3.</p>
<p id="Par45">We performed GSEA on the differentially expressed datasets using fGSEA<sup><a href="#CR91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR91">91</a></sup> to determine to what extent insulin and estrogen pathways were impacted in spaceflight. Estrogen- and insulin- specific gene pathways were selected using the Molecular Signatures Database (MSigDB)<sup><a href="#CR91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR91">91</a>,<a href="#CR92" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR92">92</a></sup>. The latest version MSigDB v2022.1 was used in the pathway search. The keyword “estrogen” was used to identify the estrogen-specific pathways through all database collections and all contributors within the <em>Mus musculus</em> species. Similarly, the keyword “insulin” was used to identify insulin-specific pathways through all contributors and <em>Mus musculus</em> database collections. The outcome of the fGSEA analysis is the normalized enrichment score (NES) of any given pathway, alongside valuable information on the analysis, such as the adjusted p-value, and the leading-edge genes of the pathway. The NES is the enrichment score normalized based on the number of genes in the gene set. It indicates the representation of the pathway genes in the dataset gene list, which is priorly ranked according to the values of the t-score of the genes. A positive NES indicates that the pathway genes are mostly represented at the top of the gene list, while a negative NES indicates that the pathway genes are mostly represented at the bottom of the gene list. The plots of normalized enrichment score (NES) of the different pathways for a given dataset are rendered using ggplot2<sup><a href="#CR93" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR93">93</a></sup>, and the heatmaps across different datasets or different genes are rendered using the ComplexHeatmap R package.</p>
<p id="Par46">We used Ingenuity Pathway Analysis (IPA) software (Ingenuity Systems) for canonical pathway analysis and subsequent predictions in each tissue with statistically significant genes with a fold-change ≥ 1.2 (and ≤ −1.2) comparing flight conditions versus habitat ground controls (FDR &lt;  0.05 as stated above). Low fold-change has become quite standard when trying to identify genes that are differentially expressed, as low cutoffs are less affected by different data normalization schemes and they are less likely to eliminate key genes operating under very tight level regulation<sup><a href="#CR61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR61">61</a></sup>. IPA was used to predict statistically significant activation or inhibition of canonical pathways using activation <em>Z</em>-score statistics (≥2, activated or ≤−2, inhibited)<sup><a href="#CR94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR94">94</a></sup>.</p></section><section id="Sec16"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Cell-Free Epigenome JAXA Study plasma cfRNA data</h3>
<p id="Par47">Aggregated normalized RNA expression values were shared through NASA’s GeneLab platform with accession number: OSD-530<sup><a href="#CR51" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR51">51</a></sup>. Blood samples were collected from 6 astronauts before, during, and after the spaceflight on the ISS. cfRNA samples were obtained by purifying total RNA from plasma samples and analyzed by RNA-seq. Mean expression values from normalized read counts of 6 astronauts were used for the analysis in this report. Heatmaps were made for the specific genes on the normalized values per time point using R package pheatmap version 1.0.12.</p></section><section id="Sec17"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Inspiration 4 (i4) astronaut sample collection</h3>
<p id="Par48">Inspiration4 was the world’s first all-civilian mission to orbit Earth. Four civilians, two males and two females, spent three days in Low-Earth Orbit (LEO) at 585 km above Earth. The mission launched from NASA Kennedy Space Center on 15 September 2021 and splashed down in the Atlantic Ocean near Cape Canaveral on September 18th, 2021. Several human-related experiments were carried out to study the effects of spaceflight on human health and performance in collaboration with SpaceX, the Translational Research Institute for Space Health (TRISH) at Baylor College of Medicine (BCM), and Weill Cornell Medicine. The experiments carried out on the Inspiration4 crew-members were performed in accordance with the relevant guidelines at the principal investigators’ institutions. Moreover, the different study designs and the corresponding methods to collect and analyze the biological samples were approved by BCM IRB. All biological data derived from the Inspiration4 mission were collected pre and post flights. For this study, only data from blood samples and skin biopsies were used. Pre-flight samples were collected at L-92, L-44, and L-3 days prior to launch to space. Upon return, post-flight samples were collected at R + 1, R + 45, and R + 82 days.</p></section><section id="Sec18"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">i4 PBMC Single-cell sequencing and analysis</h3>
<p id="Par49">Blood samples were collected before (Pre-launch: L-92, L-44, and L-3) and after (Return; R + 1, R + 45, and R + 82) the spaceflight. Chromium Next GEM Single Cell 5’ v2, 10x Genomics was used to generate single cell data from isolated PBMCs. Subpopulations were annotated based on Azimuth human PBMC ref. <sup><a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">95</a></sup>. ssGSEA was used to calculate the pathway scores of Insulin Resistance, Estrogen Signaling, Insulin Signaling. Heatmaps are generated by the pheatmap R package.</p></section><section id="Sec19"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Skin spatial transcriptomics</h3>
<p id="Par50">For skin spatial transcriptomics data, 4 mm diameter skin biopsies were obtained from all Inspiration4 crew members, once before flight and as soon as possible after return (L-44 and R + 1). These biopsies were flash frozen and processed with the NanoString GeoMx platform. A 20x scan was used to select 95 freeform regions of interest (ROIs) to guide selection of regions of interest. From immunofluorescence staining, we identified epidermis, dermis (OD), and vasculatures (VA); we subdivided epidermis into outer and inner layers, which corresponded to granular and spinous layer for outer epidermal (OE), and basal layer for inner epidermal (IE). GeoMx WTA sequencing reads from NovaSeq6000 were compiled into FASTQ files corresponding to each ROI and converted to digital count conversion files using the NanoString GeoMx NGS DnD Pipeline. From the Q3 normalized count matrix that accounts for factors such as capture area, cellularity, and read quality, the DESeq2 method was used to perform differential expression analysis.</p></section><section id="Sec20"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Proteomics sample preparation and data processing</h3>
<p id="Par51">The specific details of the proteomics method was previously published and can be found on GeneLab’s platform. In brief, after protein homogenization, solubilization, and hydrolysis, resulting peptides were dried and labeled using TMT isobaric tags. The labeled peptides were analyzed on the Orbitrap Fusion mass spectrometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific). The Fusion MS instrument was operated in positive ion data-dependent mode with synchronous precursor selection (SPS)-MS3 analysis for reporter ion quantitation. Peptide and protein identification and quantification was performed with MaxQuant (1.6.2.3) using a mouse reference database from Uniprot. Protein groups containing matches to decoys or contaminants were discarded. Subsequently, Internal Reference Scaling (IRS) method was employed to normalize protein intensities between different TMT experiments using common proteins in pooled internal standards. The data was log2 transformed and scaled by subtracting the median for each sample. Limma was employed to determine differentially protein abundance between the flight group versus the ground control. All data was plotted as heatmaps using pheatmap (ver.1.0.12).</p></section><section id="Sec21"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Statistics and reproducibility</h3>
<p id="Par52">All statistics are described throughout the methods. Briefly, for all RNA-seq data we utilized a cutoff of FDR (or adjusted <em>p</em>-value) &lt; 0.05. For pathway analysis (i.e. GSEA analysis) as indicated in the methods we used a FDR &lt; 0.25, which is the recommended statistical cutoff to utilize for GSEA analysis<sup><a href="#CR92" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR92">92</a></sup>.</p></section><section id="Sec22"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Reporting summary</h3>
<p id="Par53">Further information on research design is available in the <a href="#MOESM5" class="usa-link">Nature Portfolio Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></section></section><section id="Sec23"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Supplementary information</h2>

<p>The online version contains supplementary material available at 10.1038/s42003-023-05213-2.</p></section><section id="Bib1" class="ref-list"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">References</h2>
<section id="Bib1_sec2"><ul class="ref-list font-sm" style="list-style-type:none">
